<p><b>Newcastle upon Tyne</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small>locally </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/nj/: &#39;n&#39; in &#39;new&#39;">nj</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/əl/: &#39;le&#39; in &#39;bottle&#39;">əl</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-NewcastleUponTyne(ShortA).ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/En-uk-NewcastleUponTyne%28ShortA%29.ogg" class="internal" title="En-uk-NewcastleUponTyne(ShortA).ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-Wells2008_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wells2008-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> commonly known as <b>Newcastle</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">city</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear" title="Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East England</a>, 103 miles (166&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> and 277 miles (446&#160;km) north of <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> on the northern bank of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a>, 8.5&#160;mi (13.7&#160;km) from the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Newcastle is the most populous city in the North East, and forms the core of the <a href="/wiki/Tyneside" title="Tyneside">Tyneside</a> <a href="/wiki/Conurbation" title="Conurbation">conurbation</a>, the eighth most populous <a href="/wiki/List_of_urban_areas_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of urban areas in the United Kingdom">urban area in the United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pop_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Newcastle is a member of the English <a href="/wiki/Core_Cities_Group" title="Core Cities Group">Core Cities Group</a><sup id="cite_ref-coreCity_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coreCity-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Eurocities" title="Eurocities">Eurocities</a> network of European cities.<sup id="cite_ref-euroCity2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euroCity2-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-euroCity_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euroCity-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Newcastle was part of the county of <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a> until 1400, when it became a <a href="/wiki/County_of_itself" class="mw-redirect" title="County of itself">county of itself</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-self57567_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-self57567-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sepNewc_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sepNewc-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> a status it retained until becoming part of Tyne and Wear in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Regional_nickname" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional nickname">regional nickname</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a> for people from Newcastle and the surrounding area is <a href="/wiki/Geordie" title="Geordie">Geordie</a>. Newcastle also houses <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a>, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Russell_Group" title="Russell Group">Russell Group</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Northumbria_University" title="Northumbria University">Northumbria University</a>.
</p><p>The city developed around the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> settlement <a href="/wiki/Pons_Aelius" title="Pons Aelius">Pons Aelius</a><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and was named after the <a href="/wiki/The_Castle,_Newcastle" title="The Castle, Newcastle">castle</a> built in 1080 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert Curthose</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a>'s eldest son. The city grew as an important centre for the <a href="/wiki/Wool_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Wool trade">wool trade</a> in the 14th century, and later became a major coal mining area. The port developed in the 16th century and, along with the shipyards lower down the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a>, was amongst the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> and ship-repairing centres. 
</p><p>Newcastle's economy includes corporate headquarters, learning, digital technology, retail, tourism and cultural centres, from which the city contributes £13&#160;billion towards the United Kingdom's GVA. Among its icons are <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_United_F.C." title="Newcastle United F.C.">Newcastle United</a> <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> club and the <a href="/wiki/Tyne_Bridge" title="Tyne Bridge">Tyne Bridge</a>. Since 1981 the city has hosted the <a href="/wiki/Great_North_Run" title="Great North Run">Great North Run</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Half_marathon" title="Half marathon">half marathon</a> which attracts over 57,000 runners each year.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-51"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-52"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">15.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="History of Newcastle upon Tyne">History of Newcastle upon Tyne</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman">Roman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Roman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first recorded settlement in what is now Newcastle was <a href="/wiki/Pons_Aelius" title="Pons Aelius">Pons Aelius</a>, a Roman fort and bridge across the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a>. It was given the family name of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>, who founded it in the 2nd century AD. This rare honour suggests Hadrian may have visited the site and instituted the bridge on his tour of Britain. The population of Pons Aelius then is estimated at 2,000. Fragments of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> are visible in parts of Newcastle, particularly along the West Road. The course of the "Roman Wall" can be traced eastwards to the <a href="/wiki/Segedunum" title="Segedunum">Segedunum</a> <a href="/wiki/Castra" title="Castra">Roman fort</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wallsend" title="Wallsend">Wallsend</a>—the "wall's end"—and to the supply fort <a href="/wiki/Arbeia" title="Arbeia">Arbeia</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Shields" title="South Shields">South Shields</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The extent of Hadrian's Wall was 73 miles (117&#160;km), spanning the width of Britain; the Wall incorporated the <a href="/wiki/Vallum_(Hadrian%27s_Wall)" title="Vallum (Hadrian&#39;s Wall)">Vallum</a>, a large rearward ditch with parallel mounds,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and was built primarily for defence, to prevent unwanted <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> and the incursion of <a href="/wiki/Pict" class="mw-redirect" title="Pict">Pictish</a> tribes from the north, not as a fighting line for a major invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_schloss.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Newcastle_schloss.jpg/170px-Newcastle_schloss.jpg" width="170" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Newcastle_schloss.jpg/255px-Newcastle_schloss.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Newcastle_schloss.jpg/340px-Newcastle_schloss.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1372" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_schloss.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Castle,_Newcastle" title="The Castle, Newcastle">Newcastle Castle</a> Keep is the oldest structure in the city, dating back to at least the 11th century.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-Saxon_and_Norman">Anglo-Saxon and Norman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Anglo-Saxon and Norman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">Roman departure from Britain</a>, completed in 410, Newcastle became part of the powerful <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>, and was known throughout this period as <i>Monkchester</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Monkchester_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monkchester-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Conflicts with the <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danes</a> in 876 left the river Tyne and its settlements in ruin.<sup id="cite_ref-ncl.ac.uk_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncl.ac.uk-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> After the conflicts with the Danes, and following the <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_1088" title="Rebellion of 1088">1088 rebellion</a> against the Normans, Monkchester was all but destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Odo_of_Bayeux" title="Odo of Bayeux">Odo of Bayeux</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of its strategic position, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert Curthose</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William the Conqueror</a>, erected a wooden <a href="/wiki/The_Castle,_Newcastle" title="The Castle, Newcastle">castle</a> there in the year 1080. The town was henceforth known as <i>Novum Castellum</i> or <i>New Castle</i>. The wooden structure was replaced by a stone castle in 1087.<sup id="cite_ref-ncl.ac.uk_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncl.ac.uk-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The castle was rebuilt again in 1172 during the reign of Henry II. Much of the keep which can be seen in the city today dates from this period.<sup id="cite_ref-ncl.ac.uk_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncl.ac.uk-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, Newcastle was England's northern fortress. Incorporated first by <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a>, the city had a new charter granted by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth</a> in 1589.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> A 25-foot (7.6&#160;m) high stone <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_town_wall" title="Newcastle town wall">wall</a> was built around the town in the 13th century,<sup id="cite_ref-newc13thcent_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newc13thcent-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> to defend it from invaders during the <a href="/wiki/Border_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Border country">Border</a> war against Scotland. The Scots king <a href="/wiki/William_the_Lion" title="William the Lion">William the Lion</a> was imprisoned in Newcastle in 1174, and <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a> brought the <a href="/wiki/Stone_of_Scone" title="Stone of Scone">Stone of Scone</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> south through the town. Newcastle was successfully defended against the Scots three times during the 14th century, and was created a <a href="/wiki/County_corporate" title="County corporate">county corporate</a> with its own <a href="/wiki/Sheriff" title="Sheriff">sheriff</a> by <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV</a> in 1400.<sup id="cite_ref-self57567_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-self57567-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sepNewc_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sepNewc-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="16th_to_19th_centuries">16th to 19th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 16th to 19th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg/220px-Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg/330px-Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg/440px-Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2495" data-file-height="1686" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle-upon-Tyne_from_New_Chatham_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_T_Allom.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An engraving by <a href="/wiki/William_Miller_(engraver)" title="William Miller (engraver)">William Miller</a> of Newcastle in 1832</div></div></div>
<p>From 1530, a royal act restricted all shipments of coal from <a href="/wiki/Tyneside" title="Tyneside">Tyneside</a> to <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Quayside" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle Quayside">Newcastle Quayside</a>, giving a monopoly in the coal trade to a cartel of Newcastle burgesses known as the <a href="/wiki/The_Hostmen_of_Newcastle-upon-Tyne" class="mw-redirect" title="The Hostmen of Newcastle-upon-Tyne">Hostmen</a>. This monopoly, which lasted for a considerable time, helped Newcastle prosper and develop into a major town. The phrase <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/coals_to_Newcastle" class="extiw" title="wikt:coals to Newcastle">taking coals to Newcastle</a></i> was first recorded contextually in 1538.<sup id="cite_ref-coalsNewcastle_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coalsNewcastle-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The phrase itself means a pointless pursuit.<sup id="cite_ref-coalsToNewcastle_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coalsToNewcastle-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
In the 18th century, the American entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Dexter" title="Timothy Dexter">Timothy Dexter</a>, regarded as an eccentric, defied this idiom. He was persuaded to sail a shipment of coal to Newcastle by merchants plotting to ruin him; however, his shipment arrived on the Tyne during a strike that had crippled local production, allowing him to turn a considerable profit.<sup id="cite_ref-knapp_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knapp-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nash_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nash-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Tunnel_(Newcastle)_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Victoria_Tunnel_%28Newcastle%29_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg/220px-Victoria_Tunnel_%28Newcastle%29_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Victoria_Tunnel_%28Newcastle%29_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg/330px-Victoria_Tunnel_%28Newcastle%29_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Victoria_Tunnel_%28Newcastle%29_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg/440px-Victoria_Tunnel_%28Newcastle%29_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2878" data-file-height="2158" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Tunnel_(Newcastle)_near_Ouse_Street_2010-02-19.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Victoria_Tunnel_(Newcastle)" title="Victoria Tunnel (Newcastle)">Victoria Tunnel</a>, built 1842. In 1935 after a government document requested its cities build air-raid shelters, part of the tunnel was converted.<sup id="cite_ref-VTunnel201401_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VTunnel201401-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the Sandgate area, to the east of the city, and beside the river, resided the close-knit community of <a href="/wiki/Keelmen" title="Keelmen">keelmen</a> and their families.<sup id="cite_ref-keelmen_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keelmen-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> They were so called because they worked on the keels, boats that were used to transfer coal from the river banks to the waiting <a href="/wiki/Collier_(ship_type)" class="mw-redirect" title="Collier (ship type)">colliers</a>, for export to London and elsewhere. In the 1630s, about 7,000 out of 20,000 inhabitants of Newcastle died of <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_England" title="Black Death in England">plague</a>, more than one-third of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Specifically within the year 1636, it is roughly estimated with evidence held by the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Antiquaries_of_Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Society of Antiquaries of Newcastle upon Tyne">Society of Antiquaries</a> that 47% of the then population of Newcastle died from the epidemic; this may also have been the most devastating loss in any British city in this period.<sup id="cite_ref-Plag1636_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plag1636-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RiverTyne2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/RiverTyne2.png/220px-RiverTyne2.png" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/RiverTyne2.png/330px-RiverTyne2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/RiverTyne2.png/440px-RiverTyne2.png 2x" data-file-width="1473" data-file-height="1002" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RiverTyne2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newcastle was once a major industrial centre particularly for coal and shipping</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, the North declared for the King.<sup id="cite_ref-northCWar_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northCWar-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In a bid to gain Newcastle and the Tyne, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Cromwell's</a> allies, the Scots, captured the town of <a href="/wiki/Newburn" title="Newburn">Newburn</a>. In 1644, the Scots then captured the reinforced fortification on the Lawe in <a href="/wiki/South_Shields" title="South Shields">South Shields</a> following a siege. and the city was <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Newcastle" title="Siege of Newcastle">besieged for many months</a>. It was eventually stormed ("with roaring drummes") and sacked by Cromwell's allies. The grateful King bestowed the <a href="/wiki/Motto" title="Motto">motto</a> <i>"Fortiter Defendit Triumphans"</i> ("Triumphing by a brave defence") upon the town. Charles I was imprisoned in Newcastle by the Scots in 1646–7.<sup id="cite_ref-civilWar_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-civilWar-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 18th century, Newcastle was the country's fourth largest print centre after London, <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-printing2010-07-26_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-printing2010-07-26-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Literary_and_Philosophical_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary and Philosophical Society">Literary and Philosophical Society</a> of 1793,<sup id="cite_ref-printing2010-07-26_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-printing2010-07-26-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> with its erudite debates and large stock of books in several languages, predated the <a href="/wiki/London_Library" title="London Library">London Library</a> by half a century.<sup id="cite_ref-printing2010-07-26_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-printing2010-07-26-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Newcastle also became a glass producer with a reputation for brilliant <a href="/wiki/Flint_glass" title="Flint glass">flint glass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg/220px-Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg/330px-Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg/440px-Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="775" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_City_Centre_17.9.1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newcastle city centre, 1917</div></div></div>
<p>A permanent military presence was established in the city with the completion of <a href="/wiki/Fenham_Barracks" title="Fenham Barracks">Fenham Barracks</a> in 1806.<sup id="cite_ref-history_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_fire_of_Newcastle_and_Gateshead" title="Great fire of Newcastle and Gateshead">Great fire of Newcastle and Gateshead</a> was a tragic and spectacular series of events starting on Friday 6 October 1854, in which a substantial amount of property in the two North East of England towns was destroyed in a series of fires and an explosion which killed 53 and injured hundreds.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The status of city was granted to Newcastle on 3 June 1882. In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> and heavy engineering were central to the city's prosperity; and the city was a powerhouse of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IndRev201401_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndRev201401-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> This revolution resulted in the urbanisation of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-Urb2014-01_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urb2014-01-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 1817 the Maling company, at one time the largest pottery company in the world, moved to the city.<sup id="cite_ref-pottery_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pottery-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Victorian industrial revolution brought industrial structures that included the <span class="frac nowrap">2<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>-mile (4&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Tunnel_(Newcastle)" title="Victoria Tunnel (Newcastle)">Victoria Tunnel</a>, built in 1842, which provided underground wagon ways to the <a href="/wiki/Jetty" title="Jetty">staithes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VTunnel2014012_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VTunnel2014012-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> On 3 February 1879, Mosley Street in the city, was the first public road in the world to be lit up by the <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">incandescent lightbulb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jswan2014061_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jswan2014061-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jswan2014062_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jswan2014062-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Newcastle was one of the first cities in the world to be lit up by electric lighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Mosely_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mosely-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Innovations in Newcastle and surrounding areas included the development of <a href="/wiki/Davy_lamp" title="Davy lamp">safety lamps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephenson%27s_Rocket" title="Stephenson&#39;s Rocket">Stephenson's Rocket</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_George_Armstrong" class="mw-redirect" title="William George Armstrong">Lord Armstrong</a>'s artillery, <a href="/wiki/Be-Ro" title="Be-Ro">Be-Ro</a> flour,<sup id="cite_ref-bero2011_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bero2011-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">Joseph Swan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">electric light</a> bulbs, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Algernon_Parsons" title="Charles Algernon Parsons">Charles Parsons</a>' invention of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>, which led to the revolution of marine propulsion and the production of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical generator">cheap electricity</a>. In 1882, Newcastle became the seat of an <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Newcastle" title="Diocese of Newcastle">Anglican diocese</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Cathedral" title="Newcastle Cathedral">St. Nicholas' Church</a> becoming its cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-Cathedral1882_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cathedral1882-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_and_21st_centuries">20th and 21st centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 20th and 21st centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Newcastle's public transport system was modernised in 1901 when <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Corporation_Tramways" title="Newcastle Corporation Tramways">Newcastle Corporation Tramways</a> electric trams were introduced to the city's streets, though these were replaced gradually by trolley buses from 1935, with the tram service finally coming to an end in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-transport1901-1950_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transport1901-1950-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The city acquired its first art gallery, the <a href="/wiki/Laing_Art_Gallery" title="Laing Art Gallery">Laing Art Gallery</a> in 1904, so named after its founder Alexander Laing, a Scottish wine and spirit merchant<sup id="cite_ref-alexanderLaing13Oct1904_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alexanderLaing13Oct1904-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> who wanted to give something back to the city in which he had made his fortune. Another art gallery, the Hatton Gallery (now part of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a>), opened in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-hattonGallery1925_1_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hattonGallery1925_1-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the advent of the motor car, Newcastle's road network was improved in the early part of the 20th century, beginning with the opening of the Redheugh road bridge in 1901<sup id="cite_ref-redheughBridge1901_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redheughBridge1901-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Tyne_Bridge" title="Tyne Bridge">Tyne Bridge</a> in 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-tyneBridge1928_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tyneBridge1928-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Efforts to preserve the city's historic past were evident as long ago as 1934, when the Museum of Science and Industry opened,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> as did the John G Joicey Museum in the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Council_housing" class="mw-redirect" title="Council housing">Council housing</a> began to replace inner city slums in the 1920s, and the process continued into the 1970s, along with substantial private house building and acquisitions.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unemployment hit record heights in Newcastle during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s.
The city's last coal pit closed in 1956,<sup id="cite_ref-openCastSciCity2011_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openCastSciCity2011-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> though a temporary open cast mine was opened in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-openCastSciCity2013_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openCastSciCity2013-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The temporary open cast mine shifted 40,000 tonnes of coal, using modern techniques to reduce noise, on a part of the City undergoing redevelopment.<sup id="cite_ref-openCastSciCity2013_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openCastSciCity2013-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The slow demise of the shipyards on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a> happened in the 1970s, 1980s, and 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg/220px-Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg/330px-Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg/440px-Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2255" data-file-height="1448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_Central_from_Manors_geograph-2389652-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View northwards from the Castle Keep, towards Berwick-on-Tweed in 1954</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg/220px-Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg/330px-Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg/440px-Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2112" data-file-height="1447" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_castle_keep_across_the_River_Tyne_to_Gateshead_geograph-2613573-by-Ben-Brooksbank.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Panorama from Newcastle castle keep across the River Tyne to Gateshead in 1954</div></div></div>
<p>During the Second World War the city and surrounding area were a target for air raids as heavy industry was involved in the production of ships and armaments. The raids caused 141 deaths and 587 injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> A former French consul in Newcastle called Jacques Serre assisted the German war effort by describing important targets in the region to <a href="/wiki/Erich_Raeder" title="Erich Raeder">Admiral Raeder</a> who was the head of the German Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The public sector in Newcastle began to expand in the 1960s. The federal structure of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Durham" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Durham">University of Durham</a> was dissolved. That university's colleges in Newcastle, which had been known as King's College, became the University of Newcastle upon Tyne (now known as <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a>), which was founded in 1963,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> followed by a Newcastle Polytechnic in 1969; the latter received university status in 1992 and became the <a href="/wiki/Northumbria_University" title="Northumbria University">Northumbria University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Further efforts to preserve the city's historic past continued in the later 20th century, with the opening of Newcastle Military Vehicle Museum in 1983 and <a href="/wiki/North_Tyneside_Steam_Railway" title="North Tyneside Steam Railway">Stephenson Railway Museum</a> in 1986. The Military Vehicle museum closed in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> New developments at the turn of the 21st century included the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Life" title="Centre for Life">Life Science Centre</a> in 2000 and <a href="/wiki/Gateshead_Millennium_Bridge" title="Gateshead Millennium Bridge">Millennium Bridge</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Based at <a href="/wiki/St_James%27_Park" title="St James&#39; Park">St James' Park</a> since 1886, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_United_F.C." title="Newcastle United F.C.">Newcastle United F.C.</a> became <a href="/wiki/Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League">Football League</a> members in 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> They have won four top division titles (the first in 1905 and the most recent in 1927), six <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cups</a> (the first in 1910 and the most recent in 1955) and the <a href="/wiki/Inter-Cities_Fairs_Cup" title="Inter-Cities Fairs Cup">Inter-Cities Fairs Cup</a> in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> They broke the world transfer record in 1996 by paying £15&#160;million for <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Rovers_F.C." title="Blackburn Rovers F.C.">Blackburn Rovers</a> and <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England</a> striker <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shearer" title="Alan Shearer">Alan Shearer</a>, one of the most prolific goalscorers of that era.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017 Newcastle was the venue for the 2017 Freedom City festival. The 2017 Freedom City festival commemorated the 50 years since <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Dr Martin Luther King's</a> visit to Newcastle, where King received his honorary degree from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-freedomCity2017_1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedomCity2017_1-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-honDegreeKing_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honDegreeKing-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-freedomCity_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedomCity-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 2018 Newcastle hosted the Great Exhibition of the North, the largest event in England in 2018. The exhibition began on 22 June with an opening ceremony on the river Tyne, and ended on 9 September with the Great North Run weekend. The exhibition describes the story of the north of England through its innovators, artists, designers and businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-riverCeremonyOpeninExh2018_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riverCeremonyOpeninExh2018-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-exOfNorth2018_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exOfNorth2018-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Newcastle is situated in the <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East</a> of England, in the metropolitan county of <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear" title="Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a> and the historical and traditional county of <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>. The city is located on the northwestern bank of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a>. It is 46 miles (74&#160;km) from the Scottish border, south of Southdean.
</p><p>The ground beneath the city is formed from <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">strata</a> of the Middle Pennine Coal Measures Group—a suite of <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mudstone" title="Mudstone">mudstones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal seam">coal seams</a> which generally dip moderately eastwards. To the west of the city are the Upper Pennine Coal Measures and further west again the sandstones and mudstones of the Stainmore Formation, the local equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/Millstone_Grit" title="Millstone Grit">Millstone Grit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg/220px-Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg/330px-Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg/440px-Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2260" data-file-height="1913" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Side_-_Tyne_Bridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Side, a street in Newcastle near the Tyne Bridge</div></div></div>
<p>In large parts, Newcastle still retains a <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> street layout. Narrow alleys or '<a href="/wiki/Chare" title="Chare">chares</a>', most of which can only be traversed by foot, still exist in abundance, particularly around the <a href="/wiki/Quayside" title="Quayside">riverside</a>. Stairs from the riverside to higher parts of the city centre and the extant <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Castle_Keep" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle Castle Keep">Castle Keep</a>, originally recorded in the 14th century, remain intact in places. Close, Sandhill and <a href="/wiki/Quayside" title="Quayside">Quayside</a> contain modern buildings as well as structures dating from the 15th–18th centuries, including <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Surtees_House" title="Bessie Surtees House">Bessie Surtees House</a>, the Cooperage and Lloyds Quayside Bars, Derwentwater House and House of Tides, a restaurant situated at a Grade I-listed 16th century merchant's house at 28–30 Close.
</p><p>The city has an extensive <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">neoclassical</a> centre referred to as Tyneside Classical<sup id="cite_ref-Tyneside_Classical_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyneside_Classical-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> largely developed in the 1830s by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Grainger" title="Richard Grainger">Richard Grainger</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Dobson_(architect)" title="John Dobson (architect)">John Dobson</a>, and recently extensively restored. Broadcaster and writer <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Maconie" title="Stuart Maconie">Stuart Maconie</a> described Newcastle as England's best-looking city<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> and the German-born British scholar of architecture, <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Pevsner" title="Nikolaus Pevsner">Nikolaus Pevsner</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Pevsner2010-07-26_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pevsner2010-07-26-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> describes <a href="/wiki/Grey_Street,_Newcastle" class="mw-redirect" title="Grey Street, Newcastle">Grey Street</a> as one of the finest streets in England. The street curves down from <a href="/wiki/Grey%27s_Monument" title="Grey&#39;s Monument">Grey's Monument</a> towards the valley of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="River Tyne, England">River Tyne</a> and was voted England's finest street in 2005 in a survey of <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4" title="BBC Radio 4">BBC Radio 4</a> listeners.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In the Google Street View awards of 2010, Grey Street came 3rd in the British picturesque category.<sup id="cite_ref-google_street_view_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google_street_view-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Osborne Road came 4th in the foodie street category.<sup id="cite_ref-google_street_view_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google_street_view-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> A portion of <a href="/wiki/Grainger_Town" title="Grainger Town">Grainger Town</a> was demolished in the 1960s to make way for the <a href="/wiki/Eldon_Square_Shopping_Centre" title="Eldon Square Shopping Centre">Eldon Square</a> Shopping Centre, including all but one side of the original <a href="/wiki/Old_Eldon_Square" title="Old Eldon Square">Eldon Square</a> itself.
</p><p>Immediately to the northwest of the city centre is <a href="/wiki/Leazes_Park" title="Leazes Park">Leazes Park</a>, established in 1873<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> after a petition by 3,000 working men of the city for "ready access to some open ground for the purpose of health and recreation". Just outside one corner of this is <a href="/wiki/St_James%27_Park" title="St James&#39; Park">St James' Park</a>, the stadium home of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_United_F.C." title="Newcastle United F.C.">Newcastle United F.C.</a> which dominates the view of the city from all directions.
</p><p>Another <a href="/wiki/Green_belt" title="Green belt">green space</a> in Newcastle is the <a href="/wiki/Town_Moor,_Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Town Moor, Newcastle upon Tyne">Town Moor</a>, lying immediately north of the city centre. It is larger than London's famous <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hampstead_Heath" title="Hampstead Heath">Hampstead Heath</a> put together<sup id="cite_ref-townmoo1_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-townmoo1-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-townmoo2_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-townmoo2-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_City" title="Freedom of the City">freemen of the city</a> have the right to graze cattle on it.<sup id="cite_ref-townmoo1_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-townmoo1-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-townmoo2_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-townmoo2-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The right extends to the pitch of <a href="/wiki/St._James%27_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="St. James&#39; Park">St. James' Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_United" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle United">Newcastle United Football Club's</a> <a href="/wiki/St._James%27_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="St. James&#39; Park">ground</a>; this is not exercised, although the Freemen do collect rent for the loss of privilege. Honorary freemen include <a href="/wiki/Bob_Geldof" title="Bob Geldof">Bob Geldof</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BobGeldof2010-09-17_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BobGeldof2010-09-17-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/King_Harald_V" class="mw-redirect" title="King Harald V">King Harald V</a> of Norway,<sup id="cite_ref-KingHNorway_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KingHNorway-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Robson" title="Bobby Robson">Bobby Robson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-SirBobby2010-09-17_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SirBobby2010-09-17-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shearer" title="Alan Shearer">Alan Shearer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-AlanShearer2010-09-17_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlanShearer2010-09-17-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> the late <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a><sup id="cite_ref-Mandela2010-09-17_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandela2010-09-17-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Shakespeare_Company" title="Royal Shakespeare Company">Royal Shakespeare Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RoyalShakespeare_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RoyalShakespeare-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Hoppings" title="The Hoppings">The Hoppings</a> funfair, said to be the largest <a href="/wiki/Travelling_funfair" class="mw-redirect" title="Travelling funfair">travelling funfair</a> in Europe, is held here annually in June.<sup id="cite_ref-Hop2010-09-17_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hop2010-09-17-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg/220px-St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg" width="220" height="75" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg/330px-St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg/440px-St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1498" data-file-height="509" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St_James_Park_Newcastle_as_seen_from_south_of_the_River_Tyne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_City_Centre" title="Newcastle City Centre">Newcastle City Centre</a> from Gateshead.</div></div></div>
<p>In the southeastern corner is <a href="/wiki/Exhibition_Park,_Newcastle" title="Exhibition Park, Newcastle">Exhibition Park</a>, which contains the only remaining pavilion from the <a href="/wiki/North_East_Coast_Exhibition" title="North East Coast Exhibition">North East Coast Exhibition of 1929</a>. From 1970s until 2006 this housed the Newcastle Military Vehicle Museum; which closed in 2006. The pavilion is now being used as a <a href="/wiki/Microbrewery" title="Microbrewery">microbrewery</a> and concert venue for Wylam Brewery.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Ouseburn</dt></dl>
<p>The wooded gorge of the <a href="/wiki/Ouseburn" title="Ouseburn">Ouseburn</a> in the east of the city is known as <a href="/wiki/Jesmond_Dene" title="Jesmond Dene">Jesmond Dene</a> and forms another popular recreation area, linked by Armstrong Park and Heaton Park to the <a href="/wiki/Ouseburn_Valley" title="Ouseburn Valley">Ouseburn Valley</a>, where the river finally reaches the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="River Tyne, England">River Tyne</a>.
</p><p>The spring time dawn chorus at 55 degrees latitude has been described as one of the best in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-dawnChorus_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dawnChorus-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The dawn chorus of the <a href="/wiki/Jesmond_Dene" title="Jesmond Dene">Jesmond Dene</a> green space, has been professionally recorded and has been used in various workplace and hospital rehabilitation facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-dawnChorus_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dawnChorus-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Architecture of suburbs</dt></dl>
<p>Significant Newcastle <a href="/wiki/Housing_developments" class="mw-redirect" title="Housing developments">housing developments</a> include <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Erskine_(architect)" title="Ralph Erskine (architect)">Ralph Erskine</a>'s the <a href="/wiki/Byker_Wall" title="Byker Wall">Byker Wall</a> designed in the 1960s, and now <a href="/wiki/Listed_building" title="Listed building">Grade II* listed</a>. It is on <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>'s list of outstanding 20th-century buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Chinatown</dt></dl>
<p>Newcastle's thriving <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Newcastle" title="Chinatown, Newcastle">Chinatown</a> lies in the north-west of <a href="/wiki/Grainger_Town" title="Grainger Town">Grainger Town</a>, centred on Stowell Street. A new Chinese arch, or <i><a href="/wiki/Paifang" title="Paifang">paifang</a></i>, providing a landmark entrance, was handed over to the city with a ceremony in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The UK's first <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> village, the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Life" title="Centre for Life">Centre for Life</a> is located in the city centre close to the <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_railway_station" title="Newcastle railway station">Central railway station</a>. The village is the first step in the City Council's plans to transform Newcastle into a <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_cities,_UK&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Science cities, UK (page does not exist)">science city</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Newcastle was voted as the Best City in the North in April 2007 by a readers' online poll organised by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> newspaper—being placed ahead of <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, Manchester, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1108px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg" class="image" title="360° panoramic shot taken from the top of the Keep"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg/1100px-NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg" width="1100" height="147" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg/1650px-NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg/2200px-NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg 2x" data-file-width="14429" data-file-height="1925" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg" title="File:NewCastle-KeepTower360.jpg"> </a></div>360° panoramic shot taken from the top of the Keep</div>
</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quayside_and_bridges_on_the_Tyne">Quayside and bridges on the Tyne</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Quayside and bridges on the Tyne">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quayside,_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Quayside%2C_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg/220px-Quayside%2C_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Quayside%2C_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg/330px-Quayside%2C_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Quayside%2C_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg/440px-Quayside%2C_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4752" data-file-height="3168" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quayside,_Newcastle_upon_Tyne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Quayside" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle Quayside">Quayside</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">Tyne</a> Gorge, between Newcastle on the north bank and <a href="/wiki/Gateshead" title="Gateshead">Gateshead</a>—a separate town and borough—on the south bank, is known for a series of dramatic bridges, including the <a href="/wiki/Tyne_Bridge" title="Tyne Bridge">Tyne Bridge</a> of 1928 which was built by <a href="/wiki/Dorman_Long" title="Dorman Long">Dorman Long</a> of <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough" title="Middlesbrough">Middlesbrough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stephenson" title="Robert Stephenson">Robert Stephenson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/High_Level_Bridge" title="High Level Bridge">High Level Bridge</a> of 1849, the first road/rail bridge in the world, and the <a href="/wiki/Swing_Bridge,_River_Tyne" title="Swing Bridge, River Tyne">Swing Bridge</a> of 1876.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Large-scale <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">regeneration</a> has replaced former shipping premises with imposing new office developments; an innovative tilting bridge, the <a href="/wiki/Gateshead_Millennium_Bridge" title="Gateshead Millennium Bridge">Gateshead Millennium Bridge</a> was commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Gateshead_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Gateshead Council">Gateshead Council</a> and has integrated the older <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Quayside" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle Quayside">Newcastle Quayside</a> more closely with major cultural developments in Gateshead, including the <a href="/wiki/BALTIC_Centre_for_Contemporary_Art" class="mw-redirect" title="BALTIC Centre for Contemporary Art">BALTIC Centre for Contemporary Art</a>, the venue for the <a href="/wiki/Turner_Prize" title="Turner Prize">Turner Prize</a> 2011<sup id="cite_ref-TurnerPrize2010_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TurnerPrize2010-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Foster,_Baron_Foster_of_Thames_Bank" title="Norman Foster, Baron Foster of Thames Bank">Norman Foster</a>-designed <a href="/wiki/The_Sage_Gateshead" class="mw-redirect" title="The Sage Gateshead">The Sage Gateshead</a> music centre. The Newcastle and Gateshead Quaysides are now a thriving, cosmopolitan area with bars, restaurants and public spaces. As a tourist promotion, Newcastle and <a href="/wiki/Gateshead" title="Gateshead">Gateshead</a> have linked together under the banner "<a href="/wiki/NewcastleGateshead" title="NewcastleGateshead">NewcastleGateshead</a>", to spearhead the regeneration of the North-East. The <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a> had the temporary <a href="/wiki/Bambuco_Bridge" title="Bambuco Bridge">Bambuco Bridge</a> in 2008 for ten days; it was not made for walking, road or cycling, but was just a sculpture.
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1108px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg" class="image" title="Newcastle Quayside Seen here in 2008 on the Quayside are the Tyne Salmon Cubes; a celebration of the River Tyne Salmon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg/1100px-Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg" width="1100" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg/1650px-Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg/2200px-Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4325" data-file-height="591" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tyne_quayside_2.1.jpg" title="File:Tyne quayside 2.1.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Quayside" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle Quayside">Newcastle Quayside</a> Seen here in 2008 on the Quayside are the Tyne Salmon Cubes; a celebration of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a> Salmon<sup id="cite_ref-salmonliek_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salmonliek-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></div>
</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grainger_Town">Grainger Town</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Grainger Town">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grainger_Town" title="Grainger Town">Grainger Town</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_England,_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Newcastle_England%2C_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg/170px-Newcastle_England%2C_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg" width="170" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Newcastle_England%2C_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg/255px-Newcastle_England%2C_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Newcastle_England%2C_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg/340px-Newcastle_England%2C_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="341" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcastle_England,_Grainger_Street_c._1906.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Grainger Street about 1906</div></div></div>
<p>The historic heart of Newcastle is the Grainger Town area. Established on classical streets built by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Grainger" title="Richard Grainger">Richard Grainger</a>, a builder and developer, between 1835 and 1842, some of Newcastle upon Tyne's finest buildings and streets lie within this area of the city centre including <a href="/wiki/Grainger_Market" class="mw-redirect" title="Grainger Market">Grainger Market</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Newcastle" title="Theatre Royal, Newcastle">Theatre Royal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earl_grey" class="mw-redirect" title="Earl grey">Grey</a> Street, Grainger Street and <a href="/wiki/John_Clayton_(town_clerk)" title="John Clayton (town clerk)">Clayton</a> Street.<sup id="cite_ref-ggrmrkt2_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ggrmrkt2-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> These buildings are predominantly four stories high, with vertical dormers, domes, turrets and spikes. Richard Grainger was said to 'have found Newcastle of bricks and timber and left it in stone'.<sup id="cite_ref-GgrMrkt1_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GgrMrkt1-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Of Grainger Town's 450 buildings, 244 are <a href="/wiki/Listed_building" title="Listed building">listed</a>, of which 29 are grade I and 49 are grade II*.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg/170px-Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg/255px-Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg/340px-Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3872" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grey%27s_Monument_Newcastle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Grey's Monument closeup</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Grey%27s_Monument" title="Grey&#39;s Monument">Grey's Monument</a>, which commemorates Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Earl Grey</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act of 1832</a>, stands above Monument Metro Station and was designed and built by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hodges_Baily" title="Edward Hodges Baily">Edward Hodges Baily</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_and_Benjamin_Green" title="John and Benjamin Green">Benjamin Green</a>. Hodges, who also built Nelson's Column, designed and built the statue,<sup id="cite_ref-Baily2010-2010-07-26_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baily2010-2010-07-26-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and the monument plinth was designed and built by Benjamin Green.<sup id="cite_ref-BenjaminGreen2010-07-26_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BenjaminGreen2010-07-26-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Grainger Market replaced an earlier market originally built in 1808 called the Butcher Market.<sup id="cite_ref-Grain201308_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grain201308-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The Grainger Market itself, was opened in 1835 and was Newcastle's first indoor market.<sup id="cite_ref-ggrmrkt3_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ggrmrkt3-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> At the time of its opening in 1835 it was said to be one of the largest and most beautiful markets in Europe. The opening was celebrated with a grand dinner attended by 2000 guests, and the Laing Art Gallery has a painting of this event.<sup id="cite_ref-ggrmrkt3_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ggrmrkt3-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> With the exception of the timber roof which was destroyed by a fire in 1901 and replaced by latticed-steel arches the Market is largely in its original condition.<sup id="cite_ref-ggrmrkt3_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ggrmrkt3-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The Grainger Market architecture, like most in Grainger Town, which are either grade I or II listed, was listed grade I in 1954 by English Heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-Grain201308_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grain201308-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of the city in the 1960s saw the demolition of part of <a href="/wiki/Grainger_Town" title="Grainger Town">Grainger Town</a> as a prelude to the modernist rebuilding initiatives of <a href="/wiki/T._Dan_Smith" title="T. Dan Smith">T. Dan Smith</a>, the leader of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_City_Council" title="Newcastle City Council">Newcastle City Council</a>. A corruption scandal was uncovered involving Smith and <a href="/wiki/John_Poulson" title="John Poulson">John Poulson</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_developer" class="mw-redirect" title="Real estate developer">property developer</a> from <a href="/wiki/Pontefract" title="Pontefract">Pontefract</a>, West Yorkshire, and both were imprisoned. Echoes of the scandal were revisited in the late 1990s in the <a href="/wiki/BBC_TV" class="mw-redirect" title="BBC TV">BBC TV</a> mini-series, <i><a href="/wiki/Our_Friends_in_the_North" title="Our Friends in the North">Our Friends in the North</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newcastle_upon_Tyne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Situated in the <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/North_Pennines" title="North Pennines">North Pennines</a>, Newcastle is amongst the driest cities in the UK. Temperature extremes recorded at Newcastle Weather Centre include 32.5&#160;°C (90.5&#160;°F) during August 1990<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> down to −12.6&#160;°C (9.3&#160;°F) during January 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Newcastle can have cool to cold winters, though usually warmer than the rural areas around it, and the winters are often compensated for by warm summers, with very long daylight hours in the summer months, longer than all other major English Cities. Newcastle upon Tyne shares the same latitude as <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen,_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="Copenhagen, Denmark">Copenhagen, Denmark</a> and southern Sweden.
</p><p>The nearest weather station to provide sunshine statistics is at Durham, about 14 miles (23&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_City_Centre" title="Newcastle City Centre">Newcastle City Centre</a>. Durham's inland, less urbanised setting results in night-time temperature data about 1 degree cooler than Newcastle proper throughout the year.
</p>
